</div>
</div>
<p>GM has participated over the years in the <a href="/wiki/World_Touring_Car_Championship" title="World Touring Car Championship">World Touring Car Championship</a> (WTCC), <a href="/wiki/24_Hours_of_Le_Mans" title="24 Hours of Le Mans">24 Hours of Le Mans</a>, <a href="/wiki/NASCAR" title="NASCAR">NASCAR</a>, <a href="/wiki/SCCA" class="mw-redirect" title="SCCA">SCCA</a>, <a href="/wiki/Supercars_Championship" title="Supercars Championship">Supercars Championship</a>, and many other world venues.</p>
<p>GM's engines were highly successful in the <a href="/wiki/Indy_Racing_League" class="mw-redirect" title="Indy Racing League">Indy Racing League</a> (IRL) throughout the 1990s, winning many races in the small <a href="/wiki/V8_engine" title="V8 engine">V-8</a> class. GM has also done much work in the <a href="/wiki/Development_of_electronics_for_GM_auto_racing" title="Development of electronics for GM auto racing">development of electronics for GM auto racing</a>. An unmodified Aurora V-8 in the Aerotech, captured 47 world records, including the record for speed endurance in the Motorsports Hall of Fame of America. Recently, the <a href="/wiki/Cadillac_V-Series" title="Cadillac V-Series">Cadillac V-Series</a> has entered motorsports racing.</p>
<p>GM has also used many cars in the American racing series <a href="/wiki/NASCAR" title="NASCAR">NASCAR</a>. Currently the <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_SS" class="mw-redirect" title="Chevrolet SS">Chevrolet SS</a> is the only entry in the series, but in the past the <a href="/wiki/Pontiac_Grand_Prix" title="Pontiac Grand Prix">Pontiac Grand Prix</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buick_Regal" title="Buick Regal">Buick Regal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oldsmobile_Cutlass" title="Oldsmobile Cutlass">Oldsmobile Cutlass</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Lumina" title="Chevrolet Lumina">Chevrolet Lumina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Malibu" title="Chevrolet Malibu">Chevrolet Malibu</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Monte_Carlo" title="Chevrolet Monte Carlo">Chevrolet Monte Carlo</a> were also used. GM has won a total of 40 NASCAR <a href="/wiki/Sprint_Cup_Series" class="mw-redirect" title="Sprint Cup Series">Sprint Cup Series</a> <a href="/wiki/List_of_NASCAR_Manufacturers%27_champions" title="List of NASCAR Manufacturers' champions">manufacturer's championships</a>, including 34 with Chevrolet, the most of any make in NASCAR history, 3 with Oldsmobile, 2 with Buick, and 1 with Pontiac. GM leads all other automobile manufacturers in races won in NASCAR's premier series at 1,011. Chevrolet leads individual makes with 677 wins.</p>
<p>In Australia, there is the <a href="/wiki/V8_Supercar" class="mw-redirect" title="V8 Supercar">V8 Supercar</a> Championship which is battled out by the two main rivals of (GM) <a href="/wiki/Holden" title="Holden">Holden</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford</a>. The current <a href="/wiki/Holden" title="Holden">Holden</a> Racing Team cars are based on the <a href="/wiki/Holden_Commodore" title="Holden Commodore">Holden Commodore</a> and run a 5.0-litre V8-cylinder engine producing 635&#160;bhp (474&#160;kW). These cars have a top speed of 298&#160;km/h (185&#160;mph) and run 0–100&#160;km/h in 3.8 seconds. The <a href="/wiki/Holden_Racing_Team" title="Holden Racing Team">Holden Racing Team</a> is Australia's most successful team in Australian touring car history. In 2006 and 2007, the Drivers championship was won by the very closely linked (now defunct) <a href="/wiki/HSV_Dealer_Team" title="HSV Dealer Team">HSV Dealer Team</a>.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research_and_development">Research and development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=General_Motors&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Research and development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/General_Motors_Technical_Center" title="General Motors Technical Center">General Motors Technical Center</a> and <a href="/wiki/General_Motors_Proving_Grounds" title="General Motors Proving Grounds">General Motors Proving Grounds</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Research_and_development" title="Research and development">Research and development</a> (R&amp;D) at General Motors began organically as the continuation of such R&amp;D as the various divisions (e.g., Cadillac, Buick, Olds, Oakland) were already doing for themselves before the merger. Its character was entirely empirical; it was whatever key people in each company had been competent enough to organize and pursue. <a href="/w/index.php?title=R._S._McLaughlin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="R. S. McLaughlin (page does not exist)">R. S. McLaughlin</a>'s Carriage Company in 1876 was designing and inventing Carriage Gear. The McLaughlin Companies became General Motors of Canada Limited. <a href="/wiki/Charles_F._Kettering" title="Charles F. Kettering">Charles F. Kettering</a>'s Dayton Engineering Laboratories Company (Delco), at <a href="/wiki/Dayton,_Ohio" title="Dayton, Ohio">Dayton, Ohio</a>, was still an independent firm at this time. Its work was well known to GM central management through its relationship as a supplier and consultancy to Cadillac and Buick.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In 1916, Durant organized the United Motors Corporation as an amalgamation of parts suppliers, supplying GM and other OEMs but independent of GM.<sup id="cite_ref-Sloan1964pp24.2C97_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sloan1964pp24.2C97-85">[85]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alfred_P._Sloan" title="Alfred P. Sloan">Alfred P. Sloan</a>, head of the newly acquired <a href="/wiki/Hyatt_Roller_Bearing_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Hyatt Roller Bearing Corporation">Hyatt Roller Bearing Corporation</a>, became United Motors' CEO. United Motors acquired Delco, and Kettering began his association with Sloan. United Motors also acquired at this time the original Remy corporation<sup id="cite_ref-Sloan1964pp24.2C97_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sloan1964pp24.2C97-85">[85]</a></sup> (called the Remy Electric Company), a competitor of Delco. In 1918 General Motors bought United Motors.<sup id="cite_ref-Sloan1964pp24.2C97_85-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sloan1964pp24.2C97-85">[85]</a></sup> Various entities grew out of the original Delco and Remy, including the Dayton Metal Products Corporation, the General Motors Research Corporation, the Delco Division and Remy Electric Division of GM, Delco Remy (now <a href="/wiki/Remy_International,_Inc." class="mw-redirect" title="Remy International, Inc.">Remy International, Inc.</a>), <a href="/wiki/ACDelco" title="ACDelco">ACDelco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delco_Electronics" title="Delco Electronics">Delco Electronics</a>, and others. Today's main successor corporation is <a href="/wiki/Delphi_Automotive" title="Delphi Automotive">Delphi Automotive</a>, which nowadays is an independent parent corporation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The General Motors Research Corporation, at Dayton under Kettering, became a true automotive research center. During the next few decades it led the development of:</p>
<ul>
<li>many electrical-appliance features for cars and trucks</li>
<li>In 1911, Charles F. Kettering, with Henry M. Leland, of Dayton Engineering Laboratories Company (DELCO) invented and filed U.S. Patent 1,150,523 for the <a href="/wiki/Starter_(engine)" title="Starter (engine)">first electric starter</a> in America.</li>
<li>In 1914 Cadillac introduced the first mass-produced production V-8 in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1921, General Motors patented the use of <a href="/wiki/Tetraethyllead" title="Tetraethyllead">Tetraethyllead</a> as an antiknock agent leading to the development of higher compression engines resulting in more power and efficiency.<sup id="cite_ref-Kitman_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kitman-87">[87]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1937, Jominy &amp; Boegehold<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> of GM invented the Jominy end-quench test for <a href="/wiki/Hardenability" title="Hardenability">hardenability</a> of carbon steel, a breakthrough in <a href="/wiki/Heat_treating" title="Heat treating">heat treating</a> still in use today as <a href="/wiki/ASTM" class="mw-redirect" title="ASTM">ASTM</a> A255.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1939, GM introduced the world's first automatic transmission the Hydra-matic for the 1940 Oldsmobile and would be adopted by the auto industry later.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1962, GM introduced the first turbo charged engine in the world for a car in the Oldsmobile Cutlass Turbo-Jetfire.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1971, the <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Roving_Vehicle" title="Lunar Roving Vehicle">Lunar Rover</a>, a partnership between GM's Delco Defense Electronics Division and <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">Boeing</a> traversed the surface of the Moon.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1972, GM produced the first rear wheel Anti-lock brake system in the world for two of their cars: the Toronado and Eldorado.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 1984, Robert Lee<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> of GM invented the <a href="/wiki/Neodymium_magnet" title="Neodymium magnet">Fe<sub>14</sub>Nd<sub>2</sub>B permanent magnet</a>, fabricated by rapid solidification.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dichlorodifluoromethane" title="Dichlorodifluoromethane">dichlorodifluoromethane</a> refrigerant for <a href="/wiki/HVAC" title="HVAC">HVAC</a> and <a href="/wiki/Refrigeration" title="Refrigeration">refrigeration</a> applications (Freon, R-12; recognized today as a bad idea environmentally [being a <a href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbon" title="Chlorofluorocarbon">chlorofluorocarbon</a> (CFC)], but a technological wonder of its day)</li>
<li>commercially practical <a href="/wiki/Two-stroke_diesel_engine" title="Two-stroke diesel engine">two-stroke diesel engines</a></li>
<li>better transmissions for track-laying vehicles</li>
<li>many other advancements</li>
</ul>
<p>Although GM R&amp;D (as it is known in colloquial shorthand) began as an organization largely built around one man (Kettering), it eventually evolved into a more modern organization whose path is shaped by individuals but not dominated entirely by any of them. World War II was a turning point wherein military affairs, after mingling with the technologies of applied science for some 80 years, first started to become fundamentally reinvented by them. Civilian life, too, changed in this direction. By the 1950s, corporations such as GM and many others were facing a new era of R&amp;D, different from earlier ones. Less about genius inventors and individual inventions, and more about organizational progress and integrated systems, it raised new questions about where the <a href="/wiki/Capital_(economics)" title="Capital (economics)">capital</a> for R&amp;D would come from in an era of limitless demand for R&amp;D (although not necessarily for production). <a href="/wiki/Alfred_P._Sloan" title="Alfred P. Sloan">Alfred Sloan</a>, longtime CEO of GM (1920s to 1960s), discussed in his memoir (also considered a seminal management treatise) the relationships between government, academia, and private industry in the areas of basic science and applied science, in light of this new era.<sup id="cite_ref-Sloan1964pp249-252_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sloan1964pp249-252-95">[95]</a></sup> The views he laid out reflected (and influenced) wide consensus on these relationships that persists largely to today.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Today, GM R&amp;D, headquartered in <a href="/wiki/Warren,_Michigan" title="Warren, Michigan">Warren, Michigan</a>, is a network of six laboratories, six science offices, and collaborative relationships in over twelve countries including working relationships with universities, government groups, suppliers, and other partners from across the globe.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>On September 7, 2014, at the Intelligent Transport Systems World Congress in Detroit GM disclosed it would be introducing auto-pilot features into certain 2017 models of its cars, which would go on sale in 2016. The "super cruise" or vehicle-to-vehicle V2V technology is likely to be first introduced to the Cadillac range, enabling drivers to switch in and out of semi-automated mode.<sup id="cite_ref-GMautomated_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GMautomated-96">[96]</a></sup> In December 2016, General Motors announced it would begin testing self-driving vehicles on public roads in Michigan, after Gov. Rick Snyder signed a package of bills legalizing the operation of autonomous vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>In March 2016, General Motors bought Cruise Automation, a San Francisco self-driving vehicle start-up, to develop self-driving cars that could be used in ride-sharing fleets.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Small_car_sales">Small car sales</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=General_Motors&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Small car sales">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chevrolet_Aveo_RS_concept_front.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Chevrolet_Aveo_RS_concept_front.jpg/220px-Chevrolet_Aveo_RS_concept_front.jpg" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Chevrolet_Aveo_RS_concept_front.jpg/330px-Chevrolet_Aveo_RS_concept_front.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Chevrolet_Aveo_RS_concept_front.jpg/440px-Chevrolet_Aveo_RS_concept_front.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2332" data-file-height="1628" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chevrolet_Aveo_RS_concept_front.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Chevrolet Aveo Concept, later went into production as the Chevrolet Aveo, UK and the Chevrolet Sonic, US</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From the 1920s onward, General Motors always maintained an internal dialog about what its economy-car and small-car policies should be.<sup id="cite_ref-Sloan1964pp313-339_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sloan1964pp313-339-100">[100]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sloan1964pp440-442_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sloan1964pp440-442-101">[101]</a></sup> The economy and size considerations often naturally overlapped, although a strong distinction was always drawn in the 20th century between policies for the U.S. market and policies for other markets. Economy (in some form) always had good demand anywhere, but its definition in the U.S. was long considered different from that in other markets. In this view, "economy" in the U.S. did not mean "small" in the sense of what qualified as "small" outside the U.S. The policy discussion often focused on topics like the higher demand for truly small cars in non-U.S. markets than in the U.S., and whether it made more sense to import a car into a certain country or to build it domestically within that country, either as some variant of <a href="/wiki/Knock-down_kit" title="Knock-down kit">knockdown</a> or with truly extensive domestic sourcing.<sup id="cite_ref-Sloan1964pp313-339_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sloan1964pp313-339-100">[100]</a></sup> GM's acquisitions of Vauxhall Motors Ltd (UK, 1925)<sup id="cite_ref-Sloan1964pp313-339_100-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sloan1964pp313-339-100">[100]</a></sup> and Adam Opel AG (Germany, 1929),<sup id="cite_ref-Sloan1964pp313-339_100-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sloan1964pp313-339-100">[100]</a></sup> rather than starting new domestic companies to compete against them, were based on analyses that convinced GM managers that acquiring an existing domestic manufacturer was a better business decision.<sup id="cite_ref-Sloan1964pp313-339_100-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sloan1964pp313-339-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although GM since the 1920s has always offered economy models in the U.S. market (relative to that market's definition in any given decade), and had done research and development in the 1940s and 1950s in preparation for any potential rise of strong demand for truly small cars in the U.S. market,<sup id="cite_ref-Sloan1964pp440-442_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sloan1964pp440-442-101">[101]</a></sup> it has also been criticized over the decades for not doing enough to promote fuel efficiency in the U.S. market in the 1970s through 1990s. GM's response has been that it has always responded to market demands, and that most Americans, despite anything they said to the contrary, did not actually demand (at purchasing-decision time) small size or fuel efficiency in their vehicles to any great or lasting extent. Although some U.S. consumers flocked temporarily to the ideal of fuel economy whenever fuel supply crises arose (such as 1973 and 1979), they flocked equally enthusiastically to SUVs when cheap fuel of the 1980s and 1990s temporarily shielded them from any downside to these choices.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Since the return of high fuel prices in the 2000s and 2010s, GM's interest in truly small-car programs for the U.S. market has been renewed. As part of General Motors Company development, GM revived one of its idled U.S. factories for the production of a small car in <a href="/wiki/Orion,_Michigan" class="mw-redirect" title="Orion, Michigan">Orion, Michigan</a>, with the creation of 1,200 American jobs. This will be the first time ever that a large manufacturer produces a <a href="/wiki/Supermini" title="Supermini">supermini</a> vehicle in the United States. This retooled plant will be capable of building 160,000 cars annually, including both small and compact vehicles. Production started in late 2011 with the <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Sonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Chevrolet Sonic">Chevrolet Sonic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_initiatives">Environmental initiatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=General_Motors&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Environmental initiatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DCA_06_2012_Chevy_Volt_4035.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/DCA_06_2012_Chevy_Volt_4035.JPG/220px-DCA_06_2012_Chevy_Volt_4035.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/DCA_06_2012_Chevy_Volt_4035.JPG/330px-DCA_06_2012_Chevy_Volt_4035.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/DCA_06_2012_Chevy_Volt_4035.JPG/440px-DCA_06_2012_Chevy_Volt_4035.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1950" data-file-height="1300" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DCA_06_2012_Chevy_Volt_4035.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The 2011 <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Volt" title="Chevrolet Volt">Chevrolet Volt</a>, a plug-in electric vehicle.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2016_Chevrolet_Volt_NAIAS_2015_trimmed.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/2016_Chevrolet_Volt_NAIAS_2015_trimmed.jpg/220px-2016_Chevrolet_Volt_NAIAS_2015_trimmed.jpg" width="220" height="118" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/2016_Chevrolet_Volt_NAIAS_2015_trimmed.jpg/330px-2016_Chevrolet_Volt_NAIAS_2015_trimmed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/2016_Chevrolet_Volt_NAIAS_2015_trimmed.jpg/440px-2016_Chevrolet_Volt_NAIAS_2015_trimmed.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3317" data-file-height="1780" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2016_Chevrolet_Volt_NAIAS_2015_trimmed.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Volt_(second_generation)" title="Chevrolet Volt (second generation)">Second generation Chevrolet Volt</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>General Motors has published principles regarding the environment and maintains an extensive website to inform the public. In 2008, General Motors committed to engineering half of its manufacturing plants to be landfill-free. In order to achieve its landfill-free status, production waste is recycled or reused in the manufacturing process.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/List_of_rooftop_photovoltaic_installations" title="List of rooftop photovoltaic installations">The world's largest rooftop solar power installation</a> was installed at General Motors Spanish Zaragoza Manufacturing Plant in fall 2008. The Zaragoza solar installation has about 2,000,000 square feet (190,000&#160;m<sup>2</sup>) of roof at the plant and contains about 85,000 solar panels. The installation was created, owned and operated by Veolia Environment and Clairvoyant Energy, who lease the rooftop area from General Motors.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> In 2011, General Motors also invested $7.5 million in solar-panel provider Sunlogics, which will install solar panels on GM facilities.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>GM has long worked on <a href="/wiki/Alternative_fuel_vehicle" title="Alternative fuel vehicle">alternative-technology vehicles</a>, and has led the industry with ethanol-burning <a href="/wiki/Flexible-fuel_vehicle" title="Flexible-fuel vehicle">flexible-fuel vehicles</a> that can run on either <a href="/wiki/E85" title="E85">E85</a> (ethanol) or gasoline. The company was the first to use <a href="/wiki/Turbocharger" title="Turbocharger">turbochargers</a> and was an early proponent of <a href="/wiki/V6_engine" title="V6 engine">V6 engines</a> in the 1960s, but quickly lost interest as <a href="/wiki/Muscle_car" title="Muscle car">muscle car</a> popularity increased. They demonstrated<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gas_turbine" title="Gas turbine">gas turbine</a> vehicles powered by <a href="/wiki/Kerosene" title="Kerosene">kerosene</a>, an area of interest throughout the industry, but abandoned the alternative engine configuration in view of the <a href="/wiki/1973_oil_crisis" title="1973 oil crisis">1973 oil crisis</a>. In the 1970s and 1980s, GM pushed the benefits of <a href="/wiki/Diesel_engine" title="Diesel engine">diesel engines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cylinder_deactivation" class="mw-redirect" title="Cylinder deactivation">cylinder deactivation</a> technologies with disastrous results due to poor durability in the <a href="/wiki/Oldsmobile" title="Oldsmobile">Oldsmobile</a> diesels and drivability issues in the <a href="/wiki/Cadillac" title="Cadillac">Cadillac</a> V8-6-4 variable-cylinder engines. In 1987, GM, in conjunction with <a href="/wiki/AeroVironment" title="AeroVironment">AeroVironment</a>, built the <a href="/wiki/Sunraycer" title="Sunraycer">Sunraycer</a>, which won the inaugural <a href="/wiki/World_Solar_Challenge" title="World Solar Challenge">World Solar Challenge</a> and was a showcase of advanced technology. Much of the technology from Sunraycer found its way into the Impact prototype electric vehicle (also built by Aerovironment) and was the predecessor to the <a href="/wiki/General_Motors_EV1" title="General Motors EV1">General Motors EV1</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>GM supported a compromise version of the <a href="/wiki/Corporate_Average_Fuel_Economy" title="Corporate Average Fuel Economy">Corporate Average Fuel Economy</a> (CAFE) standard increase from 27 to 35&#160;mpg<sub>‑US</sub> (8.7 to 6.7&#160;L/100&#160;km; 32 to 42&#160;mpg<sub>‑imp</sub>), the first such increase in over 20 years.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> GM announced they will introduce more Volt-based plug-in hybrids.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hybrid_electric_vehicles">Hybrid electric vehicles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=General_Motors&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Hybrid electric vehicles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chevrolet_Tahoe_hybrid_MLB_All_Star_Game_edition_at_67_St_NYC.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Chevrolet_Tahoe_hybrid_MLB_All_Star_Game_edition_at_67_St_NYC.jpg/220px-Chevrolet_Tahoe_hybrid_MLB_All_Star_Game_edition_at_67_St_NYC.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Chevrolet_Tahoe_hybrid_MLB_All_Star_Game_edition_at_67_St_NYC.jpg/330px-Chevrolet_Tahoe_hybrid_MLB_All_Star_Game_edition_at_67_St_NYC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Chevrolet_Tahoe_hybrid_MLB_All_Star_Game_edition_at_67_St_NYC.jpg/440px-Chevrolet_Tahoe_hybrid_MLB_All_Star_Game_edition_at_67_St_NYC.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chevrolet_Tahoe_hybrid_MLB_All_Star_Game_edition_at_67_St_NYC.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Chevrolet Tahoe Hybrid</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In May 2004, GM delivered the world's first full-sized hybrid pickups, the 1/2-ton Silverado/Sierra. These <a href="/wiki/Mild_hybrid" title="Mild hybrid">mild hybrids</a> did not use electrical energy for propulsion, like GM's later designs. In 2005, the <a href="/wiki/Opel_Astra" title="Opel Astra">Opel Astra</a> <a href="/wiki/Diesel_engine" title="Diesel engine">diesel</a> <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_vehicle" title="Hybrid vehicle">Hybrid</a> <a href="/wiki/Concept_vehicle" class="mw-redirect" title="Concept vehicle">concept vehicle</a> was introduced. The 2006 <a href="/wiki/Saturn_Vue#Green_Line" title="Saturn Vue">Saturn Vue Green Line</a> was the first hybrid passenger vehicle from GM and is also a mild design. GM has hinted at new hybrid technologies to be employed that will be optimized for higher speeds in freeway driving.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>GM currently offers the <a href="/wiki/2-mode_hybrid" class="mw-redirect" title="2-mode hybrid">2-mode hybrid</a> system used by the Chevrolet Tahoe/GMC Yukon/Cadillac Escalade, and GM 1/2 half-ton pickups and will later be used on other vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>Within the framework of its vehicle electrification strategy,<sup id="cite_ref-VDI_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VDI-112">[112]</a></sup> GM introduced the <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Volt" title="Chevrolet Volt">Chevrolet Volt</a> in 2010, an electric vehicle with back-up generators powered by gasoline. The production Chevrolet Volt was available in late 2010 as a 2011 model with limited availability.<sup id="cite_ref-RSC_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RSC-113">[113]</a></sup> GM delivered the first Volt during December 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>The GM Magic Bus is a hybrid-powered bus.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="All-electric_vehicles">All-electric vehicles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=General_Motors&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: All-electric vehicles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:EV1_(6).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/EV1_%286%29.jpg/220px-EV1_%286%29.jpg" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/EV1_%286%29.jpg/330px-EV1_%286%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/EV1_%286%29.jpg/440px-EV1_%286%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="723" data-file-height="520" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:EV1_(6).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Electric_car" title="Electric car">all-electric</a> <a href="/wiki/General_Motors_EV1" title="General Motors EV1">General Motors EV1</a> was introduced in California in 1996.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chevrolet_Bolt_EV_SAO_2016_8894.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Chevrolet_Bolt_EV_SAO_2016_8894.jpg/220px-Chevrolet_Bolt_EV_SAO_2016_8894.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Chevrolet_Bolt_EV_SAO_2016_8894.jpg/330px-Chevrolet_Bolt_EV_SAO_2016_8894.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Chevrolet_Bolt_EV_SAO_2016_8894.jpg/440px-Chevrolet_Bolt_EV_SAO_2016_8894.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2400" data-file-height="1600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chevrolet_Bolt_EV_SAO_2016_8894.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Bolt_EV" class="mw-redirect" title="Chevrolet Bolt EV">Chevrolet Bolt EV</a> is scheduled to be released in California by late 2016.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Who_Killed_the_Electric_Car%3F#Batteries" title="Who Killed the Electric Car?">Who Killed the Electric Car? §&#160;Batteries</a></div>
<p>General Motors was the first company (in the modern era) to release an all-electric automobile.<sup id="cite_ref-VDI_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VDI-112">[112]</a></sup> In 1990, GM debuted the "Impact" concept car at the Los Angeles Auto Show. It was the first car with zero-emissions marketed in the US in over three decades. The Impact was eventually produced as the <a href="/wiki/EV1" class="mw-redirect" title="EV1">EV1</a> for the 1996 model year. It was available through dealers located in only a few regions (e.g., California, Arizona, Georgia). Vehicles were leased, rather than sold, to individuals. In 1999 GM decided to cease production of the vehicles. When the individual leases had expired, they declined to renew the leases or allow the lessors to purchase them. All of the EV1s were eventually returned to General Motors and, with the exception of a few which were donated to museums, all were destroyed. The documentary film <i><a href="/wiki/Who_Killed_the_Electric_Car%3F" title="Who Killed the Electric Car?">Who Killed the Electric Car?</a></i> covered the EV1 story.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The EV1's cancellation had disappointed supporters of electric vehicles. In 2010, GM debuted the <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Volt" title="Chevrolet Volt">Chevrolet Volt</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_hybrid_electric_vehicle" class="mw-redirect" title="Plug-in hybrid electric vehicle">plug-in hybrid electric vehicle</a> with back-up generators powered by gasoline (<a href="/wiki/Range_extender_(vehicle)" title="Range extender (vehicle)">range-extended electric vehicle</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-R1_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-R1-116">[116]</a></sup> General Motors has announced that it is building a prototype two-seat electric vehicle with <a href="/wiki/Segway_Inc." title="Segway Inc.">Segway</a>. An early prototype of the Personal Urban Mobility and Accessibility vehicle—dubbed <a href="/wiki/Personal_Urban_Mobility_and_Accessibility" title="Personal Urban Mobility and Accessibility">Project P.U.M.A.</a>&#160;– was presented in New York at the 2009 <a href="/wiki/New_York_International_Auto_Show" title="New York International Auto Show">New York International Auto Show</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p>In October 2011, General Motors announced the production of the <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Spark_EV" class="mw-redirect" title="Chevrolet Spark EV">Chevrolet Spark EV</a>, an <a href="/wiki/All-electric_car" class="mw-redirect" title="All-electric car">all-electric</a> version of the third generation <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Spark" title="Chevrolet Spark">Chevrolet Spark</a>, with availability limited to select U.S. and global markets. In October 2012, GM Korea announced it will start making and selling the Spark EV domestically in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> The production version was unveiled at the 2012 <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Auto_Show" class="mw-redirect" title="Los Angeles Auto Show">Los Angeles Auto Show</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT112812_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT112812-119">[119]</a></sup> Within the framework of GM's vehicle electrification strategy,<sup id="cite_ref-VDI_112-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VDI-112">[112]</a></sup> the Spark EV is the first all-electric passenger car marketed by General Motors in the U.S. since the <a href="/wiki/General_Motors_EV1" title="General Motors EV1">EV1</a> was discontinued in 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT101211_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT101211-120">[120]</a></sup> The Spark EV was released in the U.S. in selected markets in California and Oregon in June 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-EVreleased_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EVreleased-121">[121]</a></sup> Retail sales began in South Korea in October 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> GM also plans to sell the Spark EV in limited quantities in Canada and select European markets.<sup id="cite_ref-GCC112712_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GCC112712-123">[123]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p>GM began production of the <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Bolt_EV" class="mw-redirect" title="Chevrolet Bolt EV">Chevrolet Bolt EV</a> in October 2016, the first ever affordable mass market <a href="/wiki/All-electric_car" class="mw-redirect" title="All-electric car">all-electric car</a> with a range of more than 200 miles (320&#160;km). Deliveries in California are scheduled to begin in late 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-SFG02042016_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SFG02042016-125">[125]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EPAratings_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPAratings-126">[126]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> The vehicle will be launched in all 50 US states and analysts expect it to sell around 30,000 units per year, though GM itself has not confirmed these estimates. The battery pack and most drivetrain components are built by LG and assembled in GM's Lake Orion plant.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Battery_packs_for_electric_vehicles">Battery packs for electric vehicles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=General_Motors&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Battery packs for electric vehicles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>GM builds battery packs in southern <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-RSC_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RSC-113">[113]</a></sup> GM also established an <a href="/w/index.php?title=Automotive_battery_laboratory&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Automotive battery laboratory (page does not exist)">automotive battery laboratory</a> in Michigan.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> GM will be responsible for battery management systems and power electronics, thermal management, as well as the pack assembly. An existing GM facility at Brownstown Township was chosen to be upgraded as battery pack plant.<sup id="cite_ref-RSC_113-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RSC-113">[113]</a></sup> LG Chem's U.S. subsidiary, Compact Power of Troy, Michigan, has been building the prototype packs for the development vehicles and will continue to provide integration support and act as a liaison for the program.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrogen_initiative">Hydrogen initiative</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=General_Motors&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Hydrogen initiative">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DSCN1474.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/DSCN1474.JPG/220px-DSCN1474.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/DSCN1474.JPG/330px-DSCN1474.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/DSCN1474.JPG/440px-DSCN1474.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DSCN1474.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/General_Motors_Sequel" class="mw-redirect" title="General Motors Sequel">Sequel</a>, a fuel cell-powered vehicle from GM</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 1966 GM Electrovan is credited with being the first hydrogen <a href="/wiki/Fuel_cell" title="Fuel cell">fuel cell</a> car ever produced.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Though fuel cells have been around since the early 1800s, General Motors was the first to use a fuel cell to power the wheels of a vehicle.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> The economic feasibility of the technically challenging hydrogen car, and the low-cost production of hydrogen to fuel it, has also been discussed by other automobile manufacturers such as <a href="/wiki/Ford" class="mw-redirect" title="Ford">Ford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chrysler" title="Chrysler">Chrysler</a>. In June 2007, Larry Burns, vice president of research and development, said he's not yet willing to say exactly when hydrogen vehicles will be mass-produced, but he said it should happen before 2020, the year many experts have predicted. He said "I sure would be disappointed if we weren't there" before 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>On July 2, 2013, GM and Honda announced a partnership to develop fuel cell systems and hydrogen storage technologies for the 2020 time frame. GM and Honda are leaders in fuel cell technology, ranking No. 1 and No. 2, respectively, in total fuel cell patents filed between 2002 and 2012, with more than 1,200 between them according to the Clean Energy Patent Growth Index.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flexible-fuel_vehicles">Flexible-fuel vehicles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=General_Motors&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Flexible-fuel vehicles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chevrolet_Impala_FlexFuel_34_MIA_12_2008_with_logo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Chevrolet_Impala_FlexFuel_34_MIA_12_2008_with_logo.jpg/220px-Chevrolet_Impala_FlexFuel_34_MIA_12_2008_with_logo.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Chevrolet_Impala_FlexFuel_34_MIA_12_2008_with_logo.jpg/330px-Chevrolet_Impala_FlexFuel_34_MIA_12_2008_with_logo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Chevrolet_Impala_FlexFuel_34_MIA_12_2008_with_logo.jpg/440px-Chevrolet_Impala_FlexFuel_34_MIA_12_2008_with_logo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1181" data-file-height="787" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chevrolet_Impala_FlexFuel_34_MIA_12_2008_with_logo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
E85 FlexFuel <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Impala" title="Chevrolet Impala">Chevrolet Impala</a> LT 2009 (USA).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>GM produces several <a href="/wiki/Flexible-fuel_vehicle" title="Flexible-fuel vehicle">flexible-fuel vehicles</a> that can operate on <a href="/wiki/E85" title="E85">E85</a> <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">ethanol fuel</a> or gasoline, or any blend of both. Since 2006 GM started featuring a bright yellow gas cap to remind drivers of the E85 capabilities.<sup id="cite_ref-USAToday_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USAToday-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<p>GM is the leader in E85 flex fuel vehicles, with over 6 million FlexFuel vehicles on the road in the U.S. In 2010, GM pledged to have more than half of their annual vehicle production be E85 or <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a> capable by 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> As of 2012, GM offers 20 ethanol-enabled FlexFuel cars and trucks in the US, and offers more FlexFuel vehicles models than any other automaker.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philanthropy">Philanthropy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=General_Motors&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Philanthropy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/General_Motors_Foundation" title="General Motors Foundation">General Motors Foundation</a></div>
<p>Since 1994, General Motors has donated over $23 million in cash and vehicles to the <a href="/wiki/Nature_Conservancy" class="mw-redirect" title="Nature Conservancy">Nature Conservancy</a>, and funding from GM supports many conservation projects.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1996, GMC partnered with the fashion industry as a part of the <i>GM/CFDA Concept: Cure</i>, a collaboration between General Motors and the Fashion industry bringing awareness to and raising funds for breast cancer. The program involved 5 designers, each lending their artistic talents to customize 5 different vehicles. <a href="/wiki/Nicole_Miller" title="Nicole Miller">Nicole Miller</a>, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Tyler_(designer)" title="Richard Tyler (designer)">Richard Tyler</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anna_Sui" title="Anna Sui">Anna Sui</a>, <a href="/wiki/Todd_Oldham" title="Todd Oldham">Todd Oldham</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mark_Eisen&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Mark Eisen (page does not exist)">Mark Eisen</a> were tasked with transforming a <a href="/wiki/Cadillac_STS" title="Cadillac STS">Cadillac STS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buick_Riviera" title="Buick Riviera">Buick Riviera</a>, <a href="/wiki/GMC_Yukon" class="mw-redirect" title="GMC Yukon">GMC Yukon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oldsmobile_Bravada" title="Oldsmobile Bravada">Oldsmobile Bravada</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Camaro" title="Chevrolet Camaro">Chevrolet Camaro</a> Z28, respectively. The cars were then auctioned with the proceeds presented to the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Nina_Hyde&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Nina Hyde (page does not exist)">Nina Hyde</a> Center at the Greater Los Angeles Auto Show in 1997.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since 1997, the GM Foundation has been a source of funding for <a href="/wiki/Safe_Kids_USA" class="mw-redirect" title="Safe Kids USA">Safe Kids USA</a>'s "Safe Kids Buckle Up" program, a national initiative to ensure child automobile safety through education and inspection.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WebWire-SKBU_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WebWire-SKBU-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>Through 2002, the PACE Awards program, led by GM, EDS, and SUN Microsystems, has given over $1.2&#160;billion of in-kind contributions which includes computers to over 18 universities to support engineering education.<sup id="cite_ref-pace_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pace-140">[140]</a></sup> In 2009, the GM led group has helped the Pace Awards program worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-PacePartners_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PacePartners-141">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2004, GM gave $51,200,000 in cash contributions and $17,200,000 in-kind donations to charitable causes.<sup id="cite_ref-donations_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-donations-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>The General Motors Foundation (GM Foundation) receives philanthropic bequests from General Motors. It is a 501(c)(3) foundation incorporated in 1976.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Brand_reorganization">Brand reorganization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=General_Motors&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Brand reorganization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As it emerged from bankruptcy and company reorganization in 2010, GM reorganized the content and structure of its brand portfolio (its <a href="/wiki/Brand_architecture" title="Brand architecture">brand architecture</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-merriamassociates.com_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merriamassociates.com-144">[144]</a></sup> Some nameplates like Pontiac, Saturn, Hummer, and service brands like Goodwrench were discontinued. Others, like <a href="/wiki/Saab_Automobile" title="Saab Automobile">Saab</a>, were sold.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> The practice of putting the "GM Mark of Excellence" on every car, no matter what the brand, was discontinued in August 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> The company has moved from a corporate-endorsed hybrid brand architecture structure, where GM underpinned every brand to a multiple brand corporate invisible brand architecture structure.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> The company's familiar square blue "badge" has been removed from the Web site and advertising, in favor of a new, subtle all-text logo treatment on its U.S. site;<sup id="cite_ref-merriamassociates.com_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merriamassociates.com-144">[144]</a></sup> the Canadian site still retains the blue "badge".<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> In 2011, GM discontinued the Daewoo brand in South Korea and replaced it with the Chevrolet brand. <sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></p>
<p>GM describes their brand politics as having "two brands" which "will drive our global growth. They are Chevrolet, which embodies the qualities of value, reliability, performance and expressive design; and Cadillac, which creates luxury vehicles that are provocative and powerful. At the same time, the <a href="/wiki/Holden" title="Holden">Holden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buick" title="Buick">Buick</a>, GMC, Baojun, <a href="/wiki/Opel" title="Opel">Opel</a> and Vauxhall brands are being carefully cultivated to satisfy as many customers as possible in select regions."<sup id="cite_ref-GM_Stockholder-letter_2011_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GM_Stockholder-letter_2011-9">[9]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GMannualreport2011_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GMannualreport2011-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:p.182</sup></p>

<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/England" title="England"><img alt="England" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/23px-Flag_of_England.svg.png" width="23" height="14" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/35px-Flag_of_England.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/46px-Flag_of_England.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="480" /></a></span> Delphi Energy (filter factory) (–1998) - sold to <a href="/wiki/Dana_Holding_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Dana Holding Corporation">Dana</a><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States"><img alt="United States" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg.png" width="23" height="12" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg/46px-Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1235" data-file-height="650" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Allison_Transmission" title="Allison Transmission">Allison Transmission</a> (1929–2007) sold to <a href="/wiki/The_Carlyle_Group" title="The Carlyle Group">The Carlyle Group</a> and <a href="/wiki/Onex_Corporation" title="Onex Corporation">Onex Corporation</a></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States"><img alt="United States" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg.png" width="23" height="12" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg/46px-Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1235" data-file-height="650" /></a></span> New United Motor Manufacturing Inc. (<a href="/wiki/NUMMI" title="NUMMI">NUMMI</a>) (1984-2009) joint venture with <a href="/wiki/Toyota" title="Toyota">Toyota</a>, factory sold to <a href="/wiki/Tesla_Motors" class="mw-redirect" title="Tesla Motors">Tesla Motors</a></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden"><img alt="Sweden" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4c/Flag_of_Sweden.svg/23px-Flag_of_Sweden.svg.png" width="23" height="14" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4c/Flag_of_Sweden.svg/35px-Flag_of_Sweden.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4c/Flag_of_Sweden.svg/46px-Flag_of_Sweden.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1000" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Saab_Automobile" title="Saab Automobile">Saab</a> (1990–2010), sold to Dutch sports car manufacturer <a href="/wiki/Spyker_Cars_N.V." class="mw-redirect" title="Spyker Cars N.V.">Spyker Cars N.V.</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> sold to <a href="/wiki/National_Electric_Vehicle_Sweden" title="National Electric Vehicle Sweden">National Electric Vehicle Sweden</a> AB on August 31, 2012.</li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany"><img alt="Germany" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/ba/Flag_of_Germany.svg/23px-Flag_of_Germany.svg.png" width="23" height="14" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/ba/Flag_of_Germany.svg/35px-Flag_of_Germany.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/ba/Flag_of_Germany.svg/46px-Flag_of_Germany.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="600" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Opel" title="Opel">Opel</a> (1929–2017), sold to <a href="/wiki/PSA_Peugeot_Citroen" class="mw-redirect" title="PSA Peugeot Citroen">PSA Peugeot Citroen</a></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom"><img alt="United Kingdom" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png" width="23" height="12" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/ae/Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg/46px-Flag_of_the_United_Kingdom.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="600" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Vauxhall" title="Vauxhall">Vauxhall</a> (1925-2017), sold to <a href="/wiki/PSA_Peugeot_Citroen" class="mw-redirect" title="PSA Peugeot Citroen">PSA Peugeot Citroen</a></li>
</ul>

The company produces Ravon, Chevrolet and Daewoo branded vehicles.</li>
</ul>

<ul>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy"><img alt="Italy" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Flag_of_Italy_%282003-2006%29.svg/23px-Flag_of_Italy_%282003-2006%29.svg.png" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Flag_of_Italy_%282003-2006%29.svg/35px-Flag_of_Italy_%282003-2006%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Flag_of_Italy_%282003-2006%29.svg/45px-Flag_of_Italy_%282003-2006%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1000" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Fiat" class="mw-redirect" title="Fiat">Fiat</a> (2000–2005), GM owned 20 percent at one time with <a href="/wiki/Put_option" title="Put option">put option</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> The two companies continue to work together on sharing automotive platforms.</li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan"><img alt="Japan" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9e/Flag_of_Japan.svg/23px-Flag_of_Japan.svg.png" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9e/Flag_of_Japan.svg/35px-Flag_of_Japan.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9e/Flag_of_Japan.svg/45px-Flag_of_Japan.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Fuji_Heavy_Industries" title="Fuji Heavy Industries">Fuji Heavy Industries</a>, manufacturer of <a href="/wiki/Subaru" title="Subaru">Subaru</a> (1999–2006), GM owned 20 percent at one time<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan"><img alt="Japan" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9e/Flag_of_Japan.svg/23px-Flag_of_Japan.svg.png" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9e/Flag_of_Japan.svg/35px-Flag_of_Japan.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9e/Flag_of_Japan.svg/45px-Flag_of_Japan.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Isuzu" class="mw-redirect" title="Isuzu">Isuzu</a> (1971–2006), GM owned 49 percent at one time.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> The two companies continue to work together on various projects.</li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/France" title="France"><img alt="France" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/Flag_of_France.svg/23px-Flag_of_France.svg.png" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/Flag_of_France.svg/35px-Flag_of_France.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/Flag_of_France.svg/45px-Flag_of_France.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/PSA_Peugeot_Citroen" class="mw-redirect" title="PSA Peugeot Citroen">PSA Peugeot Citroen</a> (2012–2013), GM owned 7 percent of the company at one time. Following heavy losses from PSA Peugeot Citroen along with restructuring at <a href="/wiki/Opel" title="Opel">Opel</a>, GM sold its entire stake in 2013 with PSA Peugeot Citroen intending to partner with <a href="/wiki/Dongfeng_Motor" title="Dongfeng Motor">Dongfeng Motor</a>. The two companies will continue to work together on sharing automotive platforms until 2026.</li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan"><img alt="Japan" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9e/Flag_of_Japan.svg/23px-Flag_of_Japan.svg.png" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9e/Flag_of_Japan.svg/35px-Flag_of_Japan.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9e/Flag_of_Japan.svg/45px-Flag_of_Japan.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Suzuki" title="Suzuki">Suzuki</a> (1981–2008), GM owned over 20 percent at one time.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> General Motors continues to sell some Suzuki models under the Chevrolet brand.</li>
</ul>

<ul>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States"><img alt="United States" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg.png" width="23" height="12" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg/46px-Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1235" data-file-height="650" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/GM_Defense" title="GM Defense">GM Defense</a> 1950–2003 was once part of <a href="/wiki/General_Motors_Diesel_Division" title="General Motors Diesel Division">General Motors Diesel Division</a> and as <b>General Dynamics Land Systems</b> division of <a href="/wiki/General_Dynamics" title="General Dynamics">General Dynamics</a></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States"><img alt="United States" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png" width="23" height="12" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/46px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1235" data-file-height="650" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Electro_Motive_Division_of_General_Motors" class="mw-redirect" title="Electro Motive Division of General Motors">Electro Motive Division of General Motors</a> was also once part of <a href="/wiki/General_Motors_Diesel_Division" title="General Motors Diesel Division">General Motors Diesel Division</a> and now known as <a href="/wiki/Electro-Motive_Diesel" title="Electro-Motive Diesel">Electro-Motive Diesel</a></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States"><img alt="United States" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png" width="23" height="12" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/46px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1235" data-file-height="650" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Detroit_Diesel" title="Detroit Diesel">Detroit Diesel</a> sold to <a href="/wiki/Penske_Corporation" title="Penske Corporation">Penske Corporation</a>; broken up and portion sold to the former DaimlerChrysler AG (now <a href="/wiki/Daimler_AG" title="Daimler AG">Daimler AG</a>); now part of <a href="/wiki/Daimler_AG" title="Daimler AG">Daimler AG</a></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada"><img alt="Canada" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cf/Flag_of_Canada.svg/23px-Flag_of_Canada.svg.png" width="23" height="12" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cf/Flag_of_Canada.svg/35px-Flag_of_Canada.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/cf/Flag_of_Canada.svg/46px-Flag_of_Canada.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="500" /></a></span> <b>Diesel Division of General Motors of Canada Limited</b> spun off and later acquired by <a href="/wiki/General_Motors_Canada" title="General Motors Canada">General Motors Canada</a> as Diesel Division of General Motors of Canada Limited</li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States"><img alt="United States" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png" width="23" height="12" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/46px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1235" data-file-height="650" /></a></span> EDS&#160;– <a href="/wiki/Electronic_Data_Systems" title="Electronic Data Systems">Electronic Data Systems</a></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States"><img alt="United States" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg.png" width="23" height="12" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg/46px-Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1235" data-file-height="650" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Delco_Remy" class="mw-redirect" title="Delco Remy">Delco Remy</a> (1918–1994) – spun off</li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States"><img alt="United States" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg.png" width="23" height="12" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg/46px-Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1235" data-file-height="650" /></a></span> <a href="/w/index.php?title=Magnaquench&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Magnaquench (page does not exist)">Magnaquench</a> (–1994) – spun off</li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States"><img alt="United States" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg.png" width="23" height="12" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg/46px-Flag_of_the_United_States_%28Pantone%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1235" data-file-height="650" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Hughes_Electronics" class="mw-redirect" title="Hughes Electronics">Hughes Electronics</a> sold to <a href="/wiki/News_Corporation" title="News Corporation">News Corporation</a> in 2003</li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States"><img alt="United States" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png" width="23" height="12" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States.svg/46px-Flag_of_the_United_States.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1235" data-file-height="650" /></a></span> 1999 GM spun off its parts making operations as <a href="/wiki/Delphi_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Delphi Corporation">Delphi</a></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies">Controversies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=General_Motors&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Controversies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ralph_Nader_and_the_Corvair">Ralph Nader and the Corvair</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=General_Motors&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Ralph Nader and the Corvair">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:122px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1960-63_Corvair.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6c/1960-63_Corvair.jpg" width="120" height="90" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="120" data-file-height="90" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1960-63_Corvair.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1961-63 Corvair swing-axle rear suspension</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i><b><a href="/wiki/Unsafe_at_Any_Speed" title="Unsafe at Any Speed">Unsafe at Any Speed</a></b></i> by <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Nader" title="Ralph Nader">Ralph Nader</a>, published in 1965, is a pioneering book accusing <a href="/wiki/Car_manufacturers" class="mw-redirect" title="Car manufacturers">car manufacturers</a> of being slow to introduce <a href="/wiki/Safety" title="Safety">safety</a> features, and reluctant to spend money on improving safety. The subject for which the book is probably most widely known, the rear-engined GM <a href="/wiki/Chevrolet_Corvair" title="Chevrolet Corvair">Chevrolet Corvair</a>, is covered in the first chapter. It relates to the first (1960–1964) models that had a <a href="/wiki/Swing_axle" title="Swing axle">swing-axle suspension</a> design which was prone to "tuck under" in certain circumstances. In substitution for the cost-cutting lack of a front stabilizer bar (anti-roll bar), Corvairs required <a href="/wiki/Tire" title="Tire">tire</a> pressures which were outside of the tire manufacturer's recommended tolerances. The Corvair relied on an unusually high front to rear pressure differential (15psi front, 26psi rear, when cold; 18 psi and 30psi hot), and if one inflated the tires equally, as was standard practice for all other cars at the time, the result was a dangerous oversteer.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup></p>
<p>In early March 1966, several media outlets, including <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Republic" title="The New Republic">The New Republic</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, reported that GM had tried to discredit <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Nader" title="Ralph Nader">Ralph Nader</a>, hiring private detectives to tap his phones and investigate his past, and hiring prostitutes to trap him in compromising situations.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> Nader sued the company for <a href="/wiki/Invasion_of_privacy" class="mw-redirect" title="Invasion of privacy">invasion of privacy</a> and settled the case for $425,000. Nader's lawsuit against GM was ultimately decided by the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Court_of_Appeals" title="New York Court of Appeals">New York Court of Appeals</a>, whose opinion in the case expanded <a href="/wiki/Tort_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Tort law">tort law</a> to cover "overzealous surveillance".<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> Nader used the proceeds from the lawsuit to start the pro-consumer Center for Study of Responsive Law.</p>
<p>A 1972 safety commission report conducted by <a href="/wiki/Texas_A%26M_University" title="Texas A&amp;M University">Texas A&amp;M University</a> concluded that the 1960–1963 Corvair possessed no greater potential for loss of control than its contemporary competitors in extreme situations.<sup id="cite_ref-John_Braithwaite_1983._p._30_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John_Braithwaite_1983._p._30-162">[162]</a></sup> The U.S. Department of Transportation (DOT) issued a press release in 1972 describing the findings of <a href="/wiki/National_Highway_Traffic_Safety_Administration" title="National Highway Traffic Safety Administration">NHTSA</a> testing from the previous year. NHTSA had conducted a series of comparative tests in 1971 studying the handling of the 1963 Corvair and four contemporary cars—a <a href="/wiki/Ford_Falcon_(North_America)" title="Ford Falcon (North America)">Ford Falcon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plymouth_Valiant" title="Plymouth Valiant">Plymouth Valiant</a>, <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen_Beetle" title="Volkswagen Beetle">Volkswagen Beetle</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Renault_Dauphine" title="Renault Dauphine">Renault Dauphine</a>—along with a second-generation Corvair (with its completely redesigned, independent rear suspension). The 143-page report reviewed NHTSA's extreme-condition handling tests, national crash-involvement data for the cars in the test as well as General Motors' internal documentation regarding the Corvair's handling.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-163">[163]</a></sup> NHTSA went on to contract an independent advisory panel of engineers to review the tests. This review panel concluded that "the 1960–63 Corvair compares favorably with contemporary vehicles used in the tests [...] the handling and stability performance of the 1960–63 Corvair does not result in an abnormal potential for loss of control or rollover, and it is at least as good as the performance of some contemporary vehicles both foreign and domestic."</p>
<p>Former GM executive <a href="/wiki/John_DeLorean" title="John DeLorean">John DeLorean</a> asserted in his book <i>On a Clear Day You Can See General Motors</i> that Nader's criticisms were valid.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup></p>
<p>Journalist <a href="/wiki/David_E._Davis" title="David E. Davis">David E. Davis</a>, in a 2009 article in <i><a href="/wiki/Automobile_Magazine" class="mw-redirect" title="Automobile Magazine">Automobile Magazine</a></i>, noted that despite Nader's claim that swing-axle rear suspension were dangerous, <a href="/wiki/Porsche" title="Porsche">Porsche</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mercedes-Benz" title="Mercedes-Benz">Mercedes-Benz</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen" title="Volkswagen">Volkswagen</a> all used similar swing-axle concepts during that era.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Defective_ignition_system_investigation">Defective ignition system investigation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=General_Motors&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Defective ignition system investigation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/General_Motors_ignition_switch_scandal" class="mw-redirect" title="General Motors ignition switch scandal">General Motors ignition switch scandal</a></div>
<p>In May 2014 the <a href="/wiki/National_Highway_Traffic_Safety_Administration" title="National Highway Traffic Safety Administration">NHTSA</a> fined the company $35 million for failing to recall cars with faulty ignition switches for a decade, despite knowing there was a problem with the switches. Thirteen deaths were attributed to the faulty switches during the time the company failed to recall the cars. The $35 million fine was the maximum the regulator could impose. <sup id="cite_ref-GMfined_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GMfined-166">[166]</a></sup> General Motors has announced that they are also facing 79 customer lawsuits asking for as much as $10 billion for economic losses attributed to the recall.<sup id="cite_ref-GMlawsuits_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GMlawsuits-167">[167]</a></sup> As well as the Cobalts, the switches of concern had been installed in many other cars, such as the Pontiac G5, the Saturn Ion, the Chevrolet HHR, the Saturn Sky, and Pontiac Solstice. Eventually the recall involved about 2.6 million GM cars worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup></p>
